<div id="review_1465861167" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1465861167" />
    <a title="Diane Barnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7262060-diane-barnes"><img alt="Diane Barnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1329773692p2/7262060.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1465861167?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane Barnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane Barnes" href="/user/show/7262060-diane-barnes">Diane Barnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7262060-diane-barnes?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1465861167" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1159780107225980733">It took me 10 days to read this book, which is unusual for me as I can read fairly quickly, especially when I really love a book. But this one demanded to be read slowly, not only to savor the language, but to get inside the mind and heart of Emerence, which proved impossible in the end.<br/>Emerence was a servant, working for an author and her husband. When she turned the tables on them at the interview, claiming that she was in fact interviewing them ("I don't do just anyone's dirty laundry, you kn</span>
  <span id="freeText1159780107225980733" style="display:none">It took me 10 days to read this book, which is unusual for me as I can read fairly quickly, especially when I really love a book. But this one demanded to be read slowly, not only to savor the language, but to get inside the mind and heart of Emerence, which proved impossible in the end.<br />Emerence was a servant, working for an author and her husband. When she turned the tables on them at the interview, claiming that she was in fact interviewing them ("I don't do just anyone's dirty laundry, you know."), that should have been a clue that she would always be the one in control. She also worked as a caretaker for her apartment building, sweeping the streets, shoveling snow, fixing things, (in her 70's!) She took care of the sick, helped the poor, hid people during and after WWII, all without expecting or desiring any return.<br />But Emerence was no saint. She railed against God and religion, hated any type of authority, made fun of any type of art or culture, refused to accept any help to improve her lot in life, preferring to remain a person who worked with her hands.<br />Her strength and endurance were legendary with her neighbors, and her iron will and determination made her intractable. She lived alone with her secrets, and never allowed anyone through her door. A woman never to be forgotten or trifled with.<br />Magda Szabo reveals Emerence to us very slowly, in small doses, and even at the end we can't know what really made her tick. I thought it was appropriate that the narrator is unnamed, only known as "the lady writer". She was one of the few people loved by Emerence but it was not her story. Emerence controls the reader as she does everything else in her life. A great book that I won't soon forget.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1159780107225980733" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1465861167">

<div id="review_2070787800" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2070787800?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590177716" href="/book/show/22357838-the-door">review of another edition</a>

    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Teresa by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/3642045-sue">Sue</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2070787800" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12732691854556153324"><b>4.5</b><br/><br/>At some point during my reading of this, I was reminded of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9777.The_God_of_Small_Things' title='The God of Small Things by Arundhati Roy' rel='nofollow noopener'>The God of Small Things</a> in that I felt as if this too can be read on two levels and that if I knew more of the history of the country of origin (here it is Hungary), I would‚Äôve gotten even more out of the novel. Because I know basically nothing of the latter (I googled some), and even though this can be read as a character(s)-relationship study, I feel as if something essential has eluded my grasp. Yes, we are told in perfect pacing w</span>
  <span id="freeText12732691854556153324" style="display:none"><b>4.5</b><br /><br />At some point during my reading of this, I was reminded of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9777.The_God_of_Small_Things" title="The God of Small Things by Arundhati Roy" rel="nofollow noopener">The God of Small Things</a> in that I felt as if this too can be read on two levels and that if I knew more of the history of the country of origin (here it is Hungary), I would‚Äôve gotten even more out of the novel. Because I know basically nothing of the latter (I googled some), and even though this can be read as a character(s)-relationship study, I feel as if something essential has eluded my grasp. Yes, we are told in perfect pacing what Emerence has endured and how she has survived the upheavals, but there are also minor characters‚Äîsuch as Sutu and Adelka‚Äîthat might represent the accommodation and compromise Hungary was forced to negotiate during and after wartime. The dignity Emerence fights so hard to keep behind the locked door of her flat is certainly a metaphor for more than just a personal boundary.<br /><br />The narrator, a writer, has made accommodations with the government, though these are not spelled out. As she looks back (the whole book is a reflection), she realizes how unseeing and selfish she‚Äôd been toward the demanding, selfless Emerence, a character described in fable-like and mythological terms, who‚Äôd tried to teach her some difficult lessons in unorthodox ways, most importantly, how to act with unconditional love. Emerence‚Äôs demands are perhaps beyond the scope of anyone to fulfill; yet the narrator (only named near the end when Emerence calls her by an affectionate nickname) realizes she has failed the older woman, especially as Emerence had taught her what she needed to know (remember Polett?) to handle the climatic situation. (As I write this, I realize there is also a third level.)<br /><br />During my read, I went back to the powerful beginning at least twice to reread it. A scene near the end is a strong indictment against materialism, for any reason, even love. If I have any criticism, it‚Äôs that though the book isn‚Äôt long, it went on for a bit too long. Except for the aforementioned scene, the novel‚Äôs other points had already been made. Despite this one reservation and my wish that I knew more, this is the kind of novel I love, one that has me testing my own boundaries.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_448234326" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/448234326" />
    <a title="Ema" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5746897-ema"><img alt="Ema" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1394635581p2/5746897.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/448234326?book_show_action=true">Nov 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ema" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ema" href="/user/show/5746897-ema">Ema</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=read-in-2013">read-in-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=hungary">hungary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=female-authors">female-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5746897-ema?shelf=bookish-gems">bookish-gems</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer448234326" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2439269190791673799">Romanians and Hungarians have a long history of not liking each other. I'm no xenophobe and I have a few friends in Budapest, yet I was sometimes wondering: what does Hungary have to offer in terms of art, music and literature? They could say the same about Romanians, too; what the heck, we are questioning our place in art history ourselves! Well, after reading this novel, I've come to think that Hungarians do have their hidden gems, just the same as we do. Magda Szab√≥'s novel was my first foray</span>
  <span id="freeText2439269190791673799" style="display:none">Romanians and Hungarians have a long history of not liking each other. I'm no xenophobe and I have a few friends in Budapest, yet I was sometimes wondering: what does Hungary have to offer in terms of art, music and literature? They could say the same about Romanians, too; what the heck, we are questioning our place in art history ourselves! Well, after reading this novel, I've come to think that Hungarians do have their hidden gems, just the same as we do. Magda Szab√≥'s novel was my first foray into Hungarian literature and my first step towards abating my ignorance. <br /><br /><b>The Door</b> has one of the most powerful and intriguing characters I've come across until now. She is merely an illiterate caretaker named Emerence, but what a character she is! Surrounded by a shroud of mystery, endowed with mythological strength, dismissive of religion and doctors, mocker of activities that don't include physical work, but also bestower of gifts, vessel of kindness, benefactor of abandoned animals, rescuer of fugitives, possessor of a higher understanding.<br /><blockquote>She had been everyone's model, everyone's helper, the supreme exemplar. Emerence was pure and incorruptible, the better self that each and every one of us aspired to be. With her permanently veiled forehead and her face that was tranquil as a lake, she asked nothing from anyone and depended on no-one. She shouldered everyone's burden without ever speaking of her own.</blockquote> <br />I was mesmerized by Emerence from beginning till end, although I can't truly say that I loved her. Her personality is magnetic, raising conflicting feelings, impregnating the reader's memory with a powerful image that lasts. <br /><br />Her past is surrounded by mystery; the stories about her childhood resemble fairy tales and are just as unreliable. Her present life is also an enigma: she keeps the door to her apartment tightly shut, inviting no one inside her home. All her friends (and she has many) are entertained outside, on her porch. Nobody has the full picture of Emerence - <i>she must have enjoyed a quiet smile at our expense as we struggled to work out the full story, as each of us tried to match his own allotted pieces of information with those granted to others</i>. <br /><br />Almost the whole novel gravitates around Emerence; slowly, we come to know her through the words of the Lady writer who hired her. Much more than the story of a rocky relationship between two different women, <b>The Door</b> is most of all a tale of discovery and conquest of a monumental personality. Did one of them manage to possess and kneel the other? <i>I killed Emerence...</i> the woman writer confesses right at the beginning of the novel. But is everything just as simple as that? <i>I had long known that the more simple a thing was, the less likely it was to be understood</i>, Magda Szab√≥ writes, and she is right.<br /><br />I could go on an on talking about Emerence; she is truly remarkable. But it's best to discover her through the pages of this novel, which I urge you to read!<br /><br /><blockquote>I know now, what I didn't then, that affection can't always be expressed in calm, orderly, articulate ways; and that one cannot prescribe the form it should take for anyone else.</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="2439269190791673799" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText5397734273680127107" style="display:none">This was a remarkable book.<br /><br />This novel was published when the Hungarian author, Magda Szabo, was 70 years old. That gives me hope! If I can be that creative hen/if I am 70...<br /><br />A reviewer from The Scotsman had this to say in praising the book: ‚Äú‚Ä¶I picked it up without expectation. I read it with gathering intensity, and a swelling admiration. I finished it, and straightaway started to read it again‚Ä¶‚Äù<br /><br />Why would somebody start to re-read the novel? But I too went back and read the very first chapter. And I am glad I did‚Ä¶.had I not done so, I would not have gotten as full a grasp of the novel as I did. Because that first chapter ‚Äúset the table‚Äù for the entire rest of the novel. In the first chapter the narrator tells us of a recurring dream/nightmare she has about a door. The novel builds up to a point near the end of it in which the narrator, Magda, tricks the old woman, Emerence, who has come to trust and love her (and it is reciprocal) to open her door of her little cottage, in an attempt to help her‚Ä¶to save her life or so she thinks‚Ä¶ although the old woman has put her faith in Magda that she would never ever let anybody through that door. I can‚Äôt divulge anything more‚Ä¶it would be a spoiler. <br /><br />And I would not want to do that‚Ä¶you need to read this book. I read it in one afternoon and evening‚Ä¶it was mesmerizing and I needed to find out how the story ended before I went to sleep. You need to find out what was behind not only the outer door that led into the house but also the inner door of that house that led into a room that nobody had been in for years, and the belongings of which was to be bequeathed to Magda, upon Emerence‚Äôs death.<br /><br />This book was voted as ‚Äú10 Best Books of 2015‚Äù by the New York Times Book Review. That‚Äôs remarkable in that it was originally published in 1987. It took nearly 20 years for it to be published in English (translated by Len Rix, 2005, Harvill Secker) and in 2015 it was re-issued by the New York Review of Books.<br /><br />Book review by Claire Messud: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2015/02/08/books/review/the-door-by-magda-szabo.html">https://www.nytimes.com/2015/02/08/bo...</a> <br />Several reviews can be found at this link: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://bookmarks.reviews/reviews/all/the-door/">https://bookmarks.reviews/reviews/all...</a><br /><br />The NYRB edition has an introduction by Ali Smith and in her first sentence she points out a fact that really bothers me‚Ä¶"The proportion of books published in the English-speaking world that are translations, from all languages (and including best-selling crime novels), is currently about three percent.‚Äù <br />Why does that bother me? Because of her second sentence which I agree with wholeheartedly: ‚ÄúA result of this is that, unless we read other languages, we miss out on superlative novels like The Door by Magda Szabo.‚Äù<br /></span>

<div id="review_1855898770" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1855898770" />
    <a title="Erika" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23743588-erika"><img alt="Erika" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1439597923p2/23743588.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1855898770?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erika" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erika" href="/user/show/23743588-erika">Erika</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590177716" href="/book/show/22357838-the-door">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23743588-erika?shelf=books-read-in-2017">books-read-in-2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1855898770" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5625417059183032675">I finished this novel a couple of weeks ago, but haven‚Äôt had time to review it until now. Turns out that was a good thing. Two weeks ago, I would have given <i>The Door</i> three stars and moved on. It was slow. It felt a little pointless. I didn‚Äôt like the characters. <br/><br/>But, because I wanted to review it, I kept the book in the front of my mind and over time a deeper truth began to emerge for me.<br/><br/><i>The Door</i> is slow because its subject matter is difficult and slippery. It felt pointless because I didn‚Äôt wor</span>
  <span id="freeText5625417059183032675" style="display:none">I finished this novel a couple of weeks ago, but haven‚Äôt had time to review it until now. Turns out that was a good thing. Two weeks ago, I would have given <i>The Door</i> three stars and moved on. It was slow. It felt a little pointless. I didn‚Äôt like the characters. <br /><br />But, because I wanted to review it, I kept the book in the front of my mind and over time a deeper truth began to emerge for me.<br /><br /><i>The Door</i> is slow because its subject matter is difficult and slippery. It felt pointless because I didn‚Äôt work hard enough to see the point. And as for likable, this isn‚Äôt necessarily intended to be a novel where you‚Äôre rooting for someone. <br /><br />The book opens with the narrator as an old woman, talking about a reoccurring nightmare where she tries desperately to open the door to her house, but is unable to turn the knob or even speak. She then says, <blockquote>‚ÄúOnce, just once in my life, not in the cerebral anaemia of sleep but in reality, a door did stand before me. That door opened. It was opened by someone who defended her solitude and impotent misery so fiercely that she would have kept that door shut though a flaming roof crackled over her head. I alone had the power to make her open that lock‚Ä¶I believed I was godlike, all-wise, judicious, benevolent and rational. We were both wrong.‚Äù </blockquote> This novel is about the relationship between the narrator, and the woman who opened the door to her. <br /><br /><b>Not Plot Driven</b><br /><br />Magda Szabo was a bestselling author in her native Hungary and <i>The Door,</i>, originally published in 1987, was considered one of her strongest books. It‚Äôs the story of a successful writer and her larger-than-life housekeeper. Emerence is a contradiction: abusive yet gentle; irrational yet pragmatic; completely open yet obsessively private. Over the two decades that Emerence works for the narrator, the women form a sort of passionate friendship filled with drama and complexity like a relationship between teenaged girls. Things come to a head, a door is opened, and another one is closed. That‚Äôs basically all that happens. <br /><br />‚ÄúSo nu?‚Äù as my grandmother used to say.<br /><br /><b>The Doors</b><br /><br />This is a novel about doors, the physical one that secures Emerence‚Äôs apartment, and the invisible ones that are more nuanced. It‚Äôs about the doors between social classes, between artifice and truth, and between the public and private self. And, for me, the deepest realm of this odd novel is the door between who you think you are and who you actually are. <br /><br />At a pivotal moment in their friendship, the narrator betrays Emerence and even though her intentions are good, the act has horrible repercussions. She makes a choice, and for the rest of her life, she has to walk around knowing that she is a person who will make that choice and not the other. You can‚Äôt unsee that in yourself. Emerence, on the other hand, doesn‚Äôt have that interior door. She‚Äôs ridiculously difficult, but she also knows exactly who she is, and that gives her a kind of freedom. <br /><br />I ended up really liking this deep and narrow novel. <br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5625417059183032675" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2124893070" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11140671507678384805">What a brilliant, infuriating, deeply perplexing book.¬† <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/497499.The_Door' title='The Door by Magda Szab√≥' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Door</a> centers on the relationship between two very different women - the protagonist who is a writer, and her housekeeper, an older woman named Emerence.¬† A clash of values between the two provides the main conflict for this tense and elusive story: Ali Smith writes in her brief introduction, "Their relationship transforms into one full of the barbed hostilities of love."<br/><br/>Emerence - cold, strong, and fiercely, irrationally independent¬†- i</span>
  <span id="freeText11140671507678384805" style="display:none">What a brilliant, infuriating, deeply perplexing book.¬† <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/497499.The_Door" title="The Door by Magda Szab√≥" rel="nofollow noopener">The Door</a> centers on the relationship between two very different women - the protagonist who is a writer, and her housekeeper, an older woman named Emerence.¬† A clash of values between the two provides the main conflict for this tense and elusive story: Ali Smith writes in her brief introduction, "Their relationship transforms into one full of the barbed hostilities of love."<br /><br />Emerence - cold, strong, and fiercely, irrationally independent¬†- is an unforgettable character, though she doesn't feel like a real person as much as a construct; but a construct for what is the question.¬† While <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/497499.The_Door" title="The Door by Magda Szab√≥" rel="nofollow noopener">The Door</a> reads almost like a twisted fable, it's morally ambiguous to the extreme: both characters engage in destructive behavior and it's difficult at times to discern who exactly you should be sympathizing with.¬† Emerence herself feels like a (very deliberately constructed) contradiction: she abhors organized religion but appears to be the embodiment of something almost divine - there's also a question of her relationship to Hungary's shifting cultural landscape that I think could benefit from a deep dive into the sociopolitical context of this historical period.<br /><br />But though I found this book brilliant from start to finish, there was something I grew to dread about picking it up the closer I got to the end.¬† Like Emerence herself, this book is entirely devoid of warmth in a way that started to feel draining; this from someone who genuinely loves dark fiction.¬† I'm happy to have read it and am eager to read more from Szab√≥ - and from Len Rix, who did a great job with the translation - but I can't decide if this is the sort of book I'll want to revisit in a few years or whether I'm sufficiently unsettled as to appreciate it from afar without attempting a reread to reengage.¬† Time will tell.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1377504224?book_show_action=true">Aug 29, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kinga" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kinga" href="/user/show/110812-kinga">Kinga</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099470281" href="/book/show/946485.The_Door">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/110812-kinga?shelf=pub-1987">pub-1987</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1377504224" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18350460000680960240">It‚Äôs a beautiful story of a co-dependent, toxic relationship between a writer and her hired help where a weird power struggle never seems to end. Emerence, the said help, seems to be more in charge than her supposed employers. She is despotic, mysterious and strong-willed. Her employer, the writer and the narrator of this (maybe semi-autobiographical) book seems emotionally volatile which makes her susceptible to Emerence‚Äôs moods and tantrums. Emerence is not a demure servant, she comes and goes</span>
  <span id="freeText18350460000680960240" style="display:none">It‚Äôs a beautiful story of a co-dependent, toxic relationship between a writer and her hired help where a weird power struggle never seems to end. Emerence, the said help, seems to be more in charge than her supposed employers. She is despotic, mysterious and strong-willed. Her employer, the writer and the narrator of this (maybe semi-autobiographical) book seems emotionally volatile which makes her susceptible to Emerence‚Äôs moods and tantrums. Emerence is not a demure servant, she comes and goes as she pleases but sometimes she works all night, cleaning everything in sight. When the writer adopts a dog, Emerence becomes its true master, which adds more fuel to their existing jealousy. Emerence never stops being tyrannical, even in her expressions of love.<br /><br />In the very beginning the narrator tells us Emerence is dead and the she killed her. We suspect this might be a hyperbole but we are still along for the ride all the way to the heart-breaking end, slightly amazed that the narrator hints it‚Äôs the author herself talking and at the same paints such an unflattering picture of herself. I got the feeling the book was written as a form of apology and penance. As if through writing about Emerence, the narrator could finally get to know here the way she couldn‚Äôt while Emerence was still alive. Emerence is elevated here, to a semi-mythical figure, the type of ‚Äòsalt of the earth‚Äô kind of character that most writers for some reason believe are more authentic in their existence than the intellectual (don‚Äôt get me started on that trope, though). <br />Additionally, we can also interpret the entire thing as a metaphor of the oppressive communist regime, which is also a rule for any book written during an oppressive communist regime. They are always somehow about that too. <br /><br />I would recommend this book for fans of Ferrante. It‚Äôs like Ferrante but with a gloomy Eastern European sheen and none of the Southern flair.<br /> <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18350460000680960240" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Tony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33771-tony"><img alt="Tony" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1292869016p2/33771.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1335191010?book_show_action=true">Jul 14, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tony" href="/user/show/33771-tony">Tony</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590177716" href="/book/show/22357838-the-door">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=nyrb-classics">nyrb-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=hungarian">hungarian</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1335191010" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9723936658073140782">In this work of Hungarian minimalism, the narrator is Magda Szab√≥, same name, same occupation, same childless marriage. Her, but not exactly her. She is a witness.<br/><br/>She hires Emerence to be a housekeeper of sorts. Well, that's not exactly true. Emerence gives them a probationary period, then allows herself to be hired. Emerence is a tireless worker, but decidedly sets her own rules. She suffers no fools and abjures education, religion, pretense. The Magda of the novel is an award-winning writer, a</span>
  <span id="freeText9723936658073140782" style="display:none">In this work of Hungarian minimalism, the narrator is Magda Szab√≥, same name, same occupation, same childless marriage. Her, but not exactly her. She is a witness.<br /><br />She hires Emerence to be a housekeeper of sorts. Well, that's not exactly true. Emerence gives them a probationary period, then allows herself to be hired. Emerence is a tireless worker, but decidedly sets her own rules. She suffers no fools and abjures education, religion, pretense. The Magda of the novel is an award-winning writer, a church-goer, and almost pathologically self-conscious. Yet, with difficulty, there is symbiosis.<br /><br />More thoughtful readers will smell allegory perhaps, the shifting of mores in a modernizing Hungary. I liked the <i>exchange</i> that occurs, when one gives, one accepts. See, there is an importance in the smallest of things. Or we can have them so. The <i>Magda</i> of the novel says: <i>In most people there is a dull sort of shame.</i> That's when even the well-intentioned disappoint.<br /><br />Thirty pages into this novel, I ordered the only other novel by Szab√≥ that has been translated into English. Halfway through, I was certain it would be a top-ten book, maybe book of the year. Things stalled towards the end though. Even the now-obligatory anthropomorphic dog lost his enchantment. Time, as always, will tell. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9723936658073140782" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1917015807" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1917015807" />
    <a title="Laysee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4863523-laysee"><img alt="Laysee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459741824p2/4863523.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1917015807?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laysee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laysee" href="/user/show/4863523-laysee">Laysee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1917015807" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer540009132901794439"><br/><b>The Door</b> is ranked among NY Times Book Review's "Ten Best Books of 2015". Regarded as Madga Szab√≥‚Äôs best work, it is a semi-autographical account of the relationship between a female novelist and her housekeeper. It is an insightful exploration of the power of human attachment. I read it feverishly in a space of a few days but the emotional devastation I felt lingered many days after. <br/><br/>Madga Szab√≥ (1917-2007) was a prominent 20th century Hungarian novelist. Her works were censored during the Stal</span>
  <span id="freeText540009132901794439" style="display:none"><br /><b>The Door</b> is ranked among NY Times Book Review's "Ten Best Books of 2015". Regarded as Madga Szab√≥‚Äôs best work, it is a semi-autographical account of the relationship between a female novelist and her housekeeper. It is an insightful exploration of the power of human attachment. I read it feverishly in a space of a few days but the emotional devastation I felt lingered many days after. <br /><br />Madga Szab√≥ (1917-2007) was a prominent 20th century Hungarian novelist. Her works were censored during the Stalinist era between 1949 and 1956, and became known only during the last few decades of her life. The novel is better known as ‚ÄúAz ajt√≥‚Äù (1987). In 2005, it was translated by Len Rix into English as <b>The Door</b>. It was shortlisted for the Independent Foreign Prize 2006 and awarded the Oxford Weidenfeld Translation Prize. In this novel, a writer, very much like Szab√≥, finds herself suddenly restored to political favor and hires a housekeeper to clean her spacious new flat in Budapest so as to focus on writing. Allowing a stranger into one‚Äôs home is invasive but it is a nightmare when she is as unpredictable and eccentric as the elderly Emerence. The writer and her housekeeper cannot be more different from each other in terms of their religious and political leanings and value systems. The story is about the bonds they forged and their love-hate relationship. <br /><br />If I were the novelist, I would fear the housekeeper more than love her. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Emerence is a strange creature. Her life is one closed door. She lives in a flat in which windows are never open. No one is invited inside and all guests are entertained on her porch. She does not like to be praised and flies into a rage when given a gift. She seems to have a compulsive need to serve, gravitating toward wrecks of any kind - wounded animals or broken men or women. She is caring and confiding one moment and distant and hostile the next. She is a seething volcano waiting to erupt. The novelist calls her a "madwoman of mercy" and rightly so.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Feelings unrelished in the story are so raw they drip with blood. The dialogues are superbly constructed and carry a powerful emotional charge. Being in their company is like walking through a minefield. Accusations hurt like knife stabs and I reel from their viciousness. A few times after their arguments, I have to put the book aside and return when I am ready for more fire and brimstone. <br /><br />What is astounding is that all this hostility is inexplicably bound up in a paralysing kind of love that deepens over time as the nobility of Emerence's heart becomes apparent and the writer is haplessly touched. Yet, when does an attachment become too strong and where does one draw the line? How ought love to be expressed? The novelist finds out the hard way: "I know now, what I didn't then, that affection can't always be expressed in calm, orderly, articulate ways; and that one cannot prescribe the form it should take for anyone else."<br /><br />Distrust and alienation are dominant emotions, for which the door is an obvious metaphor. What makes this novel so irresistible is the secrecy surrounding Emerence's life. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ The housekeeper is trapped in the history of her early life. Her real existence lies behind a locked door, and she has no intention of ever revealing it. We see her calm, impassive exterior and know there are layers of her interior life that hide her volatility and madness. Finding the key to Emerence's past becomes the reader's quest. As Emerence‚Äôs affection for the novelist deepens, that door begins to open bit by bit only to close again on account of a perceived betrayal of trust. At a critical point in the story, we are told "The lock refuses to budge, the door stands solid, as if welded to its steel frame."  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Pride is another emotion that, like a door, keeps people out. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Emerence takes pride in her horselike capacity for work, her self-sufficiency and independence. She has an overwhelming need to preserve a veneer of respectability. Her early life is fraught with pain and disgrace. The solution to restoring honor is to build a crypt - a monument of stained glass - to bury with dignity her family members and herself. The novelist too decries her own pride on several occasions with shame. "What stupid pride takes us over at times like that?" How she regrets fueling Emerence's rage by returning the uneaten food the latter had lavishly prepared for the special guest who never shows up. Then there is the pride of the novelist receiving, at long last, a coveted writer's award that stands in her way of saving Emerence in her greatest hour of need. It is totally understandable and yet the consequences are most regrettable.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Read <b>The Door</b>. It is psychologically brilliant and grandly heartbreaking. This is my first novel by Szab√≥ and I look forward to reading more of her work.</span>
  <a data-text-id="540009132901794439" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17836885334473325605">This is a strange little book translated from Hungarian about the unlikely postwar friendship of Magda( our narrator) a writer married to an academic, and a very secretive illiterate village woman named Remenence , who though hired as a household servant, quickly has them wondering who is really in charge. Reading like a mythic tale full of symbolism , this book chosen by NYT as one of 2015's 10 Best Books (though it was originally written in 1987) is not one of those warm fuzzy books about frie</span>
  <span id="freeText17836885334473325605" style="display:none">This is a strange little book translated from Hungarian about the unlikely postwar friendship of Magda( our narrator) a writer married to an academic, and a very secretive illiterate village woman named Remenence , who though hired as a household servant, quickly has them wondering who is really in charge. Reading like a mythic tale full of symbolism , this book chosen by NYT as one of 2015's 10 Best Books (though it was originally written in 1987) is not one of those warm fuzzy books about friendship. In one sentence I'd call it a compelling haunted story of the very blurry line between what we perceive ourselves and others to be and who we really are. 4 stars , because though I enjoyed it , I really cannot think of anyone to pass it along to. Looking forward to club discussion.</span>

She won France's Prix Femina √âtranger for The Door in 2003; it is considered her most notable achievement.   <span id="freeText1363307612897336824" style="display:none">Magda Szab√≥ lauded as Hungary's foremost female writer was renowned in Western Europe for her powerful prose and memorable heroines. She won France's Prix Femina √âtranger for The Door in 2003; it is considered her most notable achievement. Written in 1987, the Door tells the story of the strange and complicated relationship between two strong women of opposing backgrounds and personalities: Magda-the intellectual,the writer; and Emerence-her mysterious housekeeper, the relentless worker.<br>Emerence is an enigma who has built up a solid barrier around herself. Her "door" is both real -for the structure she uses to shut away the outside world , and metaphoric - for the hardened exterior behind which her secrets lay.<br>The psychological plot unravels achingly slowly as Magda inches further to opening that door, exposing Emerence's painful story. How Szab√≥ presents Emerence's puzzling personality is outstanding; the rage that comes off the page, then stops in its tracks to quiet apology is a mark of Szab√≥'s unique style, one that initially took a while for me to get used to, then to admire and embrace.<br>This was a worthwhile story for its unusual heroine, one I would remember for quite some time.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1382593626i/6044523.jpg" alt=" photo image_zpsbc7344a8.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Magda Szab√≥ October 5th, 1917-November 15th, 2007 </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_701777607" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/701777607" />
    <a title="Stephen P" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11602286-stephen-p"><img alt="Stephen P" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1461732018p2/11602286.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/701777607?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen P" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen P" href="/user/show/11602286-stephen-p">Stephen P</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Stephen by: </span>
      Proustitute
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer701777607" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13691332193571697494">Magda Szabo evokes an entry into the wilderness of self inflicted avoidance and therefore exile of the self. The book is peopled with complex characters eschewing the ease of cliche and stereotype; a successful writer growing more successful at her typewriter for hours at a time; her university professor husband now disabled from lung problems. They need a housekeeper. <br/><br/>An eighty something year old woman applies for the job and arrives for the interview. She interviews them more than they her. Th</span>
  <span id="freeText13691332193571697494" style="display:none">Magda Szabo evokes an entry into the wilderness of self inflicted avoidance and therefore exile of the self. The book is peopled with complex characters eschewing the ease of cliche and stereotype; a successful writer growing more successful at her typewriter for hours at a time; her university professor husband now disabled from lung problems. They need a housekeeper. <br /><br />An eighty something year old woman applies for the job and arrives for the interview. She interviews them more than they her. The world Emergence lives in consists of hard manual labor with little to no rest. She does not consider writing, reading, attending conferences. teaching, as work. Having dipped into her new employers insecurities the power structure shifts enabling a different, complex, kind of relationship to form. The writer‚Äôs life when she is not absorbed in the world of fiction is often characterized by shallow relationships and growing busyness. While Emerence, who in this small town lives in a small flat, is the caretaker for the building in lieu of rent. This meant all the repair jobs and the constant shoveling of snow off the street and the buildings walkways. She takes care of everyone. Only allows others to visit on her front porch. No one has been allowed into her flat. What little talking she is willing to do does not include her past.<br /><br />This intriguing premise is cultivated through the filter of Szabo's intimately precise style akin to that of A.S Byatt or vice-versa. Being told in first person, the POV of the writer, I was especially geared up, loving the hum and vibrations coursing through my veins by being right there. The camera focused in close, close enough where the characters breath can mist the lens of my glasses. <br /><br />Where things went off track without a major derailment, is that the camera was pulled back. I felt I was reading a first person account seen through the camera distance of a third person rendition. It had some rises in the hillside but generally flattened out becoming all too often a telling and not a showing. Despite the excellently drawn characters, the specificity of the prose, I was disappointed until I realized I was seeing the world of this story through the eyes of a character, the woman writer, who was in many ways exiled from herself, others, and the full experience of her life. Interpreting it this way I understood the veracity of the writing and the difficulty of its goal, especially maintaining this for 261 pages. It would be similar to accompanying and listening to a passive dependent person take you on a tour of their lives. It proved trying for me. If this however was the difficult task she took on Szabo was up to it and made it happen. Skilled and forthright like Emerence she worked hard to evoke this world and completed the best job possible. <br /><br />This may well be another of my quirks and may not bother other readers. If this is the case you are in for a grand read.<br /><br />3.8/5<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13691332193571697494" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_158217'>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('MvbQMc69QbCT71fezCSm1CY1bcGE6eVSAorWC4r7iwAn0vBkC8UEN2jtc4USKAb4M00VSNesmD+Fb5TiS66/Mw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('NeT36FunRqoYEhuocRmKIRPCWNoQHnl8NUc9J8URN00gwNe9nt8DLeMQP/OvFSoNBrogU0NbBBGyon/OBEQDfg==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

<span id="freeTextContainer8893918707397761005">Magda Szab√≥ was a Hungarian writer, arguably Hungary's foremost female novelist.   <span id="freeText8893918707397761005" style="display:none">Magda Szab√≥ was a Hungarian writer, arguably Hungary's foremost female novelist. She also wrote dramas, essays, studies, memories and poetry.<br /><br />Born in Debrecen, Szab√≥ graduated at the University of Debrecen as a teacher of Latin and of Hungarian. She started working as a teacher in a Calvinist all-girl school in Debrecen and H√≥dmez≈ëv√°s√°rhely. Between 1945 and 1949 she was working in the Ministry of Religion and Education. She married the writer and translator Tibor Szobotka in 1947.<br /><br />She began her writing career as a poet, publishing her first book <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15829625.B_r_ny" title="B√°r√°ny" rel="nofollow noopener">B√°r√°ny</a>
</i> ("Lamb") in 1947, which was followed by <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22405786.Vissza_az_emberig" title="Vissza az emberig" rel="nofollow noopener">Vissza az emberig</a>
</i> ("Back to the Human") in 1949. In 1949 she was awarded the Baumgarten Prize, which was--for political reasons--withdrawn from her on the very day it was given. She was dismissed from the Ministry in the same year.<br /><br />During the establishment of Stalinist rule from 1949 to 1956, the government did not allow her works to be published. Since her unemployed husband was also stigmatized by the communist regime, she was forced to teach in an elementary school during this period.<br /><br />Her first novel, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/15717080.Fresk_" title="Fresk√≥" rel="nofollow noopener">Fresk√≥</a>
</i> ("Fresco"), written in these years was published in 1958 and achieved overwhelming success among readers. Her most widely read novel, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1845128.Abig_l" title="Abig√©l" rel="nofollow noopener">Abig√©l</a>
</i> ("Abigail", 1970), is an adventure story about a schoolgirl boarding in eastern Hungary during the war.<br /><br />She received several prizes in Hungary, and her works have been published in 42 countries. In 2003, she was the winner of the French literary prize Prix Femina √âtranger for the best foreign novel.<br /><br />Her novel <i>Abig√©l</i> was popularized through a much-loved television series in 1978. <i>Abig√©l</i> was also chosen as the sixth most popular novel at the Hungarian version of Big Read. Her three other novels that were in the top 100 are <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2534123.F_r_Elise" title="F√ºr Elise" rel="nofollow noopener">F√ºr Elise</a>
</i>, <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20284116.An_Old_Fashioned_Story" title="An Old-Fashioned Story" rel="nofollow noopener">An Old-Fashioned Story</a>
</i>, and <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9377192.The_Door" title="The Door" rel="nofollow noopener">The Door</a>
</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8893918707397761005" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
